                        <section data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Rechargeable sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered as the competitive alternative to lithium ion batteries because of some special merits, such as environment friendly, low cost and especially abundant alkali element widely distributed on earth<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, G., Blake, G. R. &amp; Palstra, T. T. M. Vacancies in functional materials for clean energy storage and harvesting: the perfect imperfection. Chem. Soc. Rev. 46, 1693–1706 (2017)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d229305e423">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhao, C. et al. Ultrahigh rate and long-life sodium-ion batteries enabled by engineered surface and near-surface reactions. Adv. Mater. 30, 1–9 (2018)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d229305e423_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Gan, Q. et al. Defect-assisted selective surface phosphorus doping to enhance rate capability of titanium dioxide for sodium ion batteries. ACS Nano 13, 9247–9258 (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d229305e426">3</a></sup>. However, there is a big challenge that the ion radius of Na ions are ~ 70% larger than that of Li ions, so finding proper electrode materials which could provide big interstitial space to accommodate sodium ions and allow reversible and rapid ion insertion/extraction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Gan, Q. et al. Defect-assisted selective surface phosphorus doping to enhance rate capability of titanium dioxide for sodium ion batteries. ACS Nano 13, 9247–9258 (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d229305e430">3</a></sup> is a challenging topic at the moment. Recently, many efforts have been made to explore advanced anode materials for sodium ion batteries. Firstly, carbonaceous material is an important choice which is very cheap however carbonaceous materials show undesired properties with low capacities and/or poor cycle performance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Pan, H., Hu, Y. S. &amp; Chen, L. Room-temperature stationary sodium-ion batteries for large-scale electric energy storage. Energy Environ. Sci. 6, 2338–2360 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d229305e434">4</a></sup>. Secondly, Ti-based materials is a promising anode materials for sodium ion batteries, including Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Li<sub>0.22</sub>Ti<sub>0.78</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, Li<sub>4</sub>Ti<sub>5</sub>O<sub>12</sub>, and titanium dioxide (TiO<sub>2</sub>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Yu, L. et al. Understanding fundamentals and reaction mechanisms of electrode materials for Na-ion batteries. Small 14, 1–22 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d229305e462">5</a></sup>. Moreover, other materials based on alloying reactions (such as Sn, Sb, P and their compounds e.g. inter-metallics, oxides, sulfides, and phosphides)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Mortazavi, M., Deng, J., Shenoy, V. B. &amp; Medhekar, N. V. Elastic softening of alloy negative electrodes for Na-ion batteries. J. Power Sources 225, 207–214 (2013)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d229305e466">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wu, L. et al. SiC-Sb-C nanocomposites as high-capacity and cycling-stable anode for sodium-ion batteries. Electrochim. Acta 87, 41–45 (2013)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d229305e466_1">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wu, L. et al. Sb-C nanofibers with long cycle life as an anode material for high-performance sodium-ion batteries. Energy Environ. Sci. 7, 323–328 (2014)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d229305e466_2">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Xiao, L. et al. High capacity, reversible alloying reactions in SnSb/C nanocomposites for Na-ion battery applications. Chem. Commun. 48, 3321–3323 (2012)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d229305e466_3">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Wu, L. et al. A tin(ii) sulfide-carbon anode material based on combined conversion and alloying reactions for sodium-ion batteries. J. Mater. Chem. A 2, 16424–16428 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d229305e469">10</a></sup> and conversion reactions (oxides and sulfides, e.g. Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, FeOOH, MoO<sub>3</sub>, CuO, Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, NiCo<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and MoS<sub>2</sub>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Klein, F., Jache, B., Bhide, A. &amp; Adelhelm, P. Conversion reactions for sodium-ion batteries. Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys. 15, 15876–15887 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d229305e495">11</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Bommier, C. &amp; Ji, X. Recent development on anodes for Na-ion batteries. Isr. J. Chem. 55, 486–507 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d229305e498">12</a></sup> attract some attentions as well. In addition, organic compounds are also potential anode materials for sodium ion batteries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Choi, A. et al. 4,4′-Biphenyldicarboxylate sodium coordination compounds as anodes for Na-ion batteries. J. Mater. Chem. A 2, 14986–14993 (2014)." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d229305e502">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen, L., Li, W., Wang, Y., Wang, C. &amp; Xia, Y. Polyimide as anode electrode material for rechargeable sodium batteries. RSC Adv. 4, 25369–25373 (2014)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d229305e502_1">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Weeraratne, K. S., Alzharani, A. A. &amp; El-Kaderi, H. M. Redox-active porous organic polymers as novel electrode materials for green rechargeable sodium-ion batteries. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces 11, 23520–23526 (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d229305e505">15</a></sup>.</p><p>Titanium dioxide (TiO<sub>2</sub>) with various polymorphs like anatase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Ling, L. et al. Quick activation of nanoporous anatase TiO2 as high-rate and durable anode materials for sodium-ion batteries. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces 9, 39432–39440 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d229305e514">16</a></sup>, rutile<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Usui, H., Yoshioka, S., Wasada, K., Shimizu, M. &amp; Sakaguchi, H. Nb-doped rutile TiO2: a potential anode material for Na-ion battery. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces 7, 6567–6573 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d229305e518">17</a></sup>, amorphous<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Bella, F. et al. Unveiling the controversial mechanism of reversible Na storage in TiO2 nanotube arrays: amorphous versus anatase TiO2. Nano Res. 10, 2891–2903 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d229305e522">18</a></sup> TiO<sub>2</sub> and TiO<sub>2</sub>-B<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Liao, J. Y., Luna, B. D. &amp; Manthiram, A. TiO2-B nanowire arrays coated with layered MoS2 nanosheets for lithium and sodium storage. J. Mater. Chem. A 4, 801–806 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d229305e531">19</a></sup> as anode materials have been investigated, and it is generally considered as a promising energy storage material because of the low cost, environmental friendliness, intrinsic safety, abundant resources, high power density and long cycle life<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Zhang, Y. et al. Graphene-rich wrapped petal-like rutile TiO2 tuned by carbon dots for high-performance sodium storage. Adv. Mater. 28, 9391–9399 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d229305e535">20</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Guo, S., Yi, J., Sun, Y. &amp; Zhou, H. Recent advances in titanium-based electrode materials for stationary sodium-ion batteries. Energy Environ. Sci. 9, 2978–3006 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d229305e538">21</a></sup>. Nevertheless, the big limitation of using TiO<sub>2</sub> as anode for sodium ion batteries is its inherent low electrical conductivity. In order to solve this problem, firstly, surface coating could be a good method to improve the TiO<sub>2</sub> conductivity, especially introducing carbon additives to enhance the electrical conductivity of TiO<sub>2</sub> anodes, like amorphous carbon, CNTs and graphene have been widely studied as conductive agents<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yang, Y. et al. Carbon dots supported upon N-doped TiO2 nanorods applied into sodium and lithium ion batteries. J. Mater. Chem. A 3, 5648–5655 (2015)." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d229305e549">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhao, Q., Bi, R., Cui, J., Yang, X. &amp; Zhang, L. TiO2–x nanocages anchored in N-doped carbon fiber films as a flexible anode for high-energy sodium-ion batteries. ACS Appl. Energy Mater. 1, 4459–4466 (2018)." href="#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d229305e549_1">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Cha, H. A., Jeong, H. M. &amp; Kang, J. K. Nitrogen-doped open pore channeled graphene facilitating electrochemical performance of TiO2 nanoparticles as an anode material for sodium ion batteries. J. Mater. Chem. A 2, 5182–5186 (2014)." href="#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d229305e549_2">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang, J. et al. N-doped carbon coated anatase TiO2 nanoparticles as superior Na-ion battery anodes. J. Colloid Interface Sci. 517, 134–143 (2018)." href="#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d229305e549_3">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yeo, Y., Jung, J. W., Park, K. &amp; Kim, I. D. Graphene-wrapped anatase TiO2 nanofibers as high-rate and long-cycle-life anode material for sodium ion batteries. Sci. Rep. 5, 1–9 (2015)." href="#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d229305e549_4">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Zhang, Y., Wang, C., Hou, H., Zou, G. &amp; Ji, X. Nitrogen doped/carbon tuning yolk-Like TiO2 and its remarkable impact on sodium storage performances. Adv. Energy Mater. 7, 1600173 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d229305e552">27</a></sup>. Secondly, designing nanosized TiO<sub>2</sub>, for example nanotubes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Xiong, H., Slater, M. D., Balasubramanian, M., Johnson, C. S. &amp; Rajh, T. Amorphous TiO2 Nanotube anode for rechargeable sodium ion batteries. J. Phys. Chem. Lett. 2, 2560–2565 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d229305e558">28</a></sup>, nanoparticles<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Wu, L., Buchholz, D., Bresser, D., Gomes Chagas, L. &amp; Passerini, S. Anatase TiO2 nanoparticles for high power sodium-ion anodes. J. Power Sources 251, 379–385 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d229305e562">29</a></sup>, nanorods<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Xiong, H., Slater, M. D., Balasubramanian, M., Johnson, C. S. &amp; Rajh, T. Amorphous TiO2 Nanotube anode for rechargeable sodium ion batteries. J. Phys. Chem. Lett. 2, 2560–2565 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d229305e566">28</a></sup> and nanofiber<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Zhang, Y. et al. Boosting sodium storage in TiF3/carbon core/sheath nanofibers through an efficient mixed-conducting network. Adv. Energy Mater. 1901470, 1–9 (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d229305e570">30</a></sup>, petal-like TiO<sub>2</sub><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Zhang, Y. et al. Graphene-rich wrapped petal-like rutile TiO2 tuned by carbon dots for high-performance sodium storage. Adv. Mater. 28, 9391–9399 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d229305e576">20</a></sup>, is also a good way because the nanosized structure could effectively enlarge the active area of the material and shorten the ion diffusion path during electrochemical processes. He et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="He, H. et al. Structure-dependent performance of TiO2/C as anode material for Na-ion batteries. Nano Energy 44, 217–227 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d229305e580">31</a></sup> reported a hierarchical rod-in-tube TiO<sub>2</sub> with a uniform carbon coating as the anode material for sodium-ion batteries by a facile solvothermal method. The author claimed that this unique structure consists of a tunable nanorod core, interstitial hollow spaces, and a functional nanotube shell assembled from two-dimensional nanosheets. What’s more, heteroatom doping like N, S, B, P or Nb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang, H. et al. N-doped TiO2 with a disordered surface layer fabricated via plasma treatment as an anode with clearly enhanced performance for rechargeable sodium ion batteries. Sustain. Energy Fuels 3, 2688–2696 (2019)." href="#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d229305e586">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wu, Y., Liu, X., Yang, Z., Gu, L. &amp; Yu, Y. Nitrogen-doped ordered mesoporous anatase TiO2 nanofibers as anode materials for high performance sodium-ion batteries. Small 12, 3522–3529 (2016)." href="#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d229305e586_1">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ni, J. et al. Self-supported nanotube arrays of sulfur-doped TiO2 enabling ultrastable and robust sodium storage. Adv. Mater. 28, 2259–2265 (2016)." href="#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d229305e586_2">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="He, H. et al. N-doped rutile TiO2/C with significantly enhanced Na storage capacity for Na-ion batteries. Electrochim. Acta 236, 43–52 (2017)." href="#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d229305e586_3">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yan, D. et al. Enhanced electrochemical performances of anatase TiO2 nanotubes by synergetic doping of Ni and N for sodium-ion batteries. Electrochim. Acta 254, 130–139 (2017)." href="#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d229305e586_4">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liu, S., Cai, Z., Zhou, J., Pan, A. &amp; Liang, S. Nitrogen-doped TiO2 nanospheres for advanced sodium-ion battery and sodium-ion capacitor applications. J. Mater. Chem. A 4, 18278–18283 (2016)." href="#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d229305e586_5">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang, B. et al. Boron-doped anatase TiO2 as a high-performance anode material for sodium-ion batteries. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces 8, 16009–16015 (2016)." href="#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d229305e586_6">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Cao, M. et al. Phosphorus-doped TiO2-B nanowire arrays boosting robust pseudocapacitive properties for lithium storage. J. Power Sources 396, 327–334 (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d229305e589">39</a></sup>, which could substantially increase the electronic conductivity and the specific capacities for the sodium storage. A nitrogen-doped carbon layer coated yolk-like TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode could offer a superior high capacity of 115.9&nbsp;mAh/g<sup>1</sup> at 20&nbsp;C (6700&nbsp;mA/g)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Zhang, Y., Wang, C., Hou, H., Zou, G. &amp; Ji, X. Nitrogen doped/carbon tuning yolk-Like TiO2 and its remarkable impact on sodium storage performances. Adv. Energy Mater. 7, 1600173 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d229305e598">27</a></sup>. It is clear that the key factor to improve the electrochemical performance of TiO<sub>2</sub> is to combine strategies of shortening sodium-ion diffusion distance and improving electronic conductivity. In addition, the produced oxygen vacancies or trivalent titanium species can be formed to improve the electrical conductivity as well<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Gan, Q. et al. Defect-assisted selective surface phosphorus doping to enhance rate capability of titanium dioxide for sodium ion batteries. ACS Nano 13, 9247–9258 (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d229305e604">3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Wang, H. et al. N-doped TiO2 with a disordered surface layer fabricated via plasma treatment as an anode with clearly enhanced performance for rechargeable sodium ion batteries. Sustain. Energy Fuels 3, 2688–2696 (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d229305e607">32</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Wang, Q. et al. Synergistic effect of N-doping and rich oxygen vacancies induced by nitrogen plasma endows TiO2 superior sodium storage performance. Electrochim. Acta 309, 242–252 (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d229305e610">40</a></sup>. Wang and co-workers proposed a novel and facile N<sub>2</sub> plasma assisted annealing strategy, by which nitrogen heteroatoms and rich oxygen vacancies are incorporated into the TiO<sub>2</sub> crystal simultaneously, leading to highly enhanced electronic conductivity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Wang, Q. et al. Synergistic effect of N-doping and rich oxygen vacancies induced by nitrogen plasma endows TiO2 superior sodium storage performance. Electrochim. Acta 309, 242–252 (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d229305e619">40</a></sup>. In addition, previous work of our group has already shown that plasma treatment is a promising method to enhance the performance of TiO<sub>2</sub> as anode material. A hydrogen plasma treated TiO<sub>2</sub> as anode for lithium ion batteries shows that the treated black TiO<sub>2</sub> shows great improvement for fast lithium storage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Yan, Y. et al. Understanding the fast lithium storage performance of hydrogenated TiO2 nanoparticles. J. Mater. Chem. A 1, 14507–14513 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d229305e629">41</a></sup>. Furthermore, another work shows that nitrogen plasma treated TiO<sub>2</sub> with a disordered surface layer and nitrogen doping shows an enhanced performance for sodium ion batteries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Wang, H. et al. N-doped TiO2 with a disordered surface layer fabricated via plasma treatment as an anode with clearly enhanced performance for rechargeable sodium ion batteries. Sustain. Energy Fuels 3, 2688–2696 (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d229305e636">32</a></sup>.</p><p>Here, we developed a combination of hydrogen and nitrogen plasma assisted strategy to synthesize nitrogen doping and defect-rich&nbsp;(oxygen vacancies) TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode that demonstrated an enhanced performance as anode material of sodium ion batteries. In our research, there are three electrodes which are tested, pristine commercial TiO<sub>2</sub> as control sample (named as P-TiO<sub>2</sub>), high-power hydrogen plasma treated TiO<sub>2</sub> (named as H-TiO<sub>2</sub>) and high-power nitrogen plasma treated H-TiO<sub>2</sub> (named as H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub>). When used as anode materials, the H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub> shows the best sodium storage performance, and both&nbsp;H-N-TiO<sub>2</sub> and H-TiO<sub>2</sub> demonstrated much higher specific capacities than pristine TiO<sub>2</sub>, and also the long-term performance results are promising. The high-resolution images of transmission electron microscopy shown that there is a disordered surface layer was formed after the plasma treated materials, no matter the hydrogen or nitrogen plasma process. For this improvement of the electrodes, we could attribute it to the disordered surface layer, the oxygen vacancies and the nitrogen doping, and all of them play significant roles in enhancing the electrochemical sodium storage performance.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Experimental section"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Experimental section</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sample preparation</h3><p>The sample preparation process is similar with our previous work<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Wang, H. et al. N-doped TiO2 with a disordered surface layer fabricated via plasma treatment as an anode with clearly enhanced performance for rechargeable sodium ion batteries. Sustain. Energy Fuels 3, 2688–2696 (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d229305e678">32</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Wang, H. et al. Disordered surface formation of WS2 Via hydrogen plasma with enhanced anode performances for lithium and sodium ion batteries. Sustain. Energy Fuels 3, 865–874 (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d229305e681">42</a></sup>. Commercial TiO<sub>2</sub> (P25) nanoparticles were purchased from Sigma-Adlrich and used without further purification. 0.15&nbsp;g TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles were dispersed in 30&nbsp;ml ethanol with ultrasonic support and drop-casted onto a 6-inch Si wafer. The drop-casting process was repeated several times to achieve TiO<sub>2</sub> mass loading of about 0.8&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> (sample mass loading should be no more than 1.5&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> to avoid inhomogeneous plasma treatment). Before each drop, the previous dropped ethanol must be volatilized totally so that a homogenous distribution of the nanoparticles on the silicon wafer can be obtained.</p><p>The drop-casted wafer was transferred into a chamber for the plasma treatment, and the instrument of inductively coupled plasma enhanced chemical vapor deposition (ICP-CVD, Plasmalab 100, Oxford) was used. Before the plasma treatment process, a preconditioning process takes place at 300&nbsp;°C to ensure the quality of plasma treatment. Then H<sub>2</sub> plasma treatment was performed at 300&nbsp;°C for 30&nbsp;min, the ICP power was 3000&nbsp;W, chamber pressure was 3.52–3.76&nbsp;Pa, and H<sub>2</sub> flow rate was 50&nbsp;sccm. Afterwards, N<sub>2</sub> plasma treatment was performed at 300&nbsp;°C for 30&nbsp;min, the ICP power was 3000&nbsp;W, the chamber pressure was 3.52–3.76&nbsp;Pa, and N<sub>2</sub> flow rate was 30 sccm. After all plasma treatment, H-TiO<sub>2</sub> and H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles were obtained and scratched from the Si wafer for further investigations and application measurements.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Characterization</h3><p>The crystal structure of the TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles was characterized by X-ray diffraction (XRD, SIEMENS D5000) with Cu Kα radiation. The sample was scanned from 2θ = 10°–80° at a rate of 0.03° s<sup>-1</sup> in Bragg–Brentano geometry. Then, transmission electron microscopy (TEM, Tecnai F20) was used to characterize the nanoparticle morphology and microstructure. Ultraviolet–visible (UV–Vis) absorption spectra were measured by using a Cary 5000 UV–Vis-NIR. Finally, the samples were analyzed by XPS using a spectrometer (PHI Quantera SXM) with monochromatized Al-Kα radiation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Electrochemical experiments</h3><p>The electrochemical performances of the materials used as anode for sodium-ion batteries were tested via 2032 coin half-cells, which were assembled in an argon-filled glove box, where both the moisture and oxygen contents were less than 0.5&nbsp;ppm. The working electrode was immersed in a mixture, which consist of active material, super carbon black and polyvinylidene in ratio of 70: 15: 15, toward forming a homogeneous slurry in N-methyl-pyrrolidone (NMP). Then, the slurry was pasted onto stainless steel foil with a sample loading of about 1.8&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. The handled electrode was dried for 12&nbsp;h at 120&nbsp;°C under vacuum. Glass fiber (GF/D, Whatman) and pure sodium foil (Aldrich) were respectively used as a separator and the counter electrode. The electrolyte was 1&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in a mixture of ethylene carbonate and diethyl carbonate, in a ratio of 50:50. For the galvanostatic measurement, a battery tester (Neware, Shenzhen, China) was used. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) was applied in a potential range from 3.0 to 0.01&nbsp;V at a scan rate of 0.5&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> by a potentiostat (VMP3, BioLogics, France). Furthermore, the alternating current (AC) impedance of the samples were determined by a same potentiostat and the impedance spectra, which were acquired via a sine wave with an amplitude of 5.0&nbsp;mV in a frequency range from 100&nbsp;kHz to 0.01&nbsp;Hz after discharging/charging for 5 cycles at a current density of 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Results and discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec6-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec6">Results and discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec6-content"><p>Figure&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#Fig1">1</a> shows the high-resolution TEM images of the pristine TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles and the TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles after plasma treatments. We could see that all TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles were highly crystallized, and the interplane spacing of ordered lattices is measured to be closed to 0.35&nbsp;nm, which could be attributed to (101) planes of the anatase crystal phase. Moreover, we could find that the crystal lines of plasma treated material (H-TiO<sub>2</sub> and H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub>) do not arrange clearly compared with the pristine TiO<sub>2</sub>, and there are some crossovers of the fringes which may be a partially dislocation or defects in the TiO<sub>2</sub>. What’s more, a disordered layer was found in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#Fig1">1</a>b, c, and the thickness of the disordered surface layer is about 1.5&nbsp;nm, we think this is due to the high-power plasma treatment processes. According to previous report<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Yan, Y. et al. Slightly hydrogenated TiO2 with enhanced photocatalytic performance. J. Mater. Chem. A 2, 12708–12716 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d229305e770">43</a></sup>, the thickness of the disordered surface layer could be increase upon the plasma treatment time, after 20&nbsp;min treatment, the thickness reaches to around 1.5–2.2&nbsp;nm and the Electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) result indicated that hydrogen plasma treatment is very effective to produce Ti<sup>3+</sup> species and oxygen vacancies, which resulted in the disordered surface layer formation.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Figure 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-020-68838-x/MediaObjects/41598_2020_68838_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig1" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-020-68838-x/MediaObjects/41598_2020_68838_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure 1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="693"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>High resolution TEM images of (<b>a</b>) the pristine (P-TiO<sub>2</sub>) nanoparticles, (<b>b</b>) the nanoparticles treated with H<sub>2</sub> plasma (H-TiO<sub>2</sub>), and (c) the nanoparticles treated with both H<sub>2</sub> and N<sub>2</sub> plasma (H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 1" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
<p>The XRD patterns of the samples are shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#Fig2">2</a>a, from which we could see there are two different phases of TiO<sub>2</sub>, anatase and rutile. The peaks appearing at 2θ = 25.4°, 36.0°, 37.8°, 38.5°, 48.0°, 55.0°, 62.7°, 70.2°, 75.1° and 76.0° correspond to the (101), (103), (004), (112), (200), (211), (204), (220), (215) and (301) diffractions of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> (PDF No. 21-1272), respectively. And the other peaks which located at 2θ = 27.3°, 36.0°, 41.2°, 42.5°, 54.0°, 56.6° and 68.8° could be attributed to the (110), (101), (111), (210), (105), (220) and (301) diffractions of rutile TiO<sub>2</sub> (PDF No. 21-1276), respectively. The intensities of the peaks in the P-TiO<sub>2</sub> are slightly higher than those in the plasma treated materials. Except these small differences, all the diffraction peaks are almost the same. In addition, the peaks of nitrides were not clearly observed in the patterns, probably due to the low amount of doping contents and also the homogeneous distribution of N in the sample<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Bineesh, K. V., Kim, D.-K. &amp; Park, D.-W. Synthesis and characterization of zirconium-doped mesoporous nano-crystalline TiO2. Nanoscale 2, 1222–1228 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d229305e820">44</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Zhang, Z. L. et al. Enhancement of perovskite solar cells efficiency using N-doped TiO2 nanorod arrays as electron transfer layer. Nanoscale Res. Lett. 12, 43 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d229305e823">45</a></sup>. The UV–Vis absorbance of the samples was investigated and Tauc plots were calculated to determine the band gap energy of the materials. As shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#Fig2">2</a>b, P-TiO<sub>2</sub> shows the absorption edge at around 427&nbsp;nm, while after plasma treatment, the absorption edge shifted to ~ 685&nbsp;nm for both H-TiO<sub>2</sub> and H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub>. The strongly enhanced absorption of the plasma treated materials in the region of visible light indicates that the high-power plasma treatment can result in a reduction effect and oxygen vacancies are formed on the materials surface<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Yan, Y. et al. Understanding the fast lithium storage performance of hydrogenated TiO2 nanoparticles. J. Mater. Chem. A 1, 14507–14513 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d229305e836">41</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Yan, Y. et al. Slightly hydrogenated TiO2 with enhanced photocatalytic performance. J. Mater. Chem. A 2, 12708–12716 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d229305e839">43</a></sup>. Tauc plots (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#Fig2">2</a>c) clarifies the decline of band gap energy of the materials from 2.86 to 2.29&nbsp;eV due to the hydrogenation process<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Yan, Y. et al. Understanding the fast lithium storage performance of hydrogenated TiO2 nanoparticles. J. Mater. Chem. A 1, 14507–14513 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d229305e847">41</a></sup> and nitrogen doping<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hu, Y. et al. Synthesis and photoelectrochemical response of CdS quantum dot-sensitized TiO2 nanorod array photoelectrodes. Nanoscale Res. Lett. 8, 1–5 (2013)." href="#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d229305e851">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yao, X., Wang, X., Su, L., Yan, H. &amp; Yao, M. Band structure and photocatalytic properties of N/Zr co-doped anatase TiO2 from first-principles study. J. Mol. Catal. A Chem. 351, 11–16 (2011)." href="#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d229305e851_1">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Huang, D. G. et al. Synthesis of samarium- and nitrogen-co-doped TiO2 by modified hydrothermal method and its photocatalytic performance for the degradation of 4-chlorophenol. J. Phys. Chem. Solids 70, 853–859 (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d229305e854">48</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Figure 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-020-68838-x/MediaObjects/41598_2020_68838_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig2" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-020-68838-x/MediaObjects/41598_2020_68838_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure 2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="576"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) X-ray diffraction patterns of P-TiO<sub>2</sub>, H-TiO<sub>2</sub> and H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub>; (<b>b</b>) UV–Vis absorption spectra of P-TiO<sub>2</sub>, H-TiO<sub>2</sub> and H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub> and (<b>c</b>) Tauc plots to obtain the band gap.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 2" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
<p>XPS was used to investigate the surface chemical structure details of the obtained samples (shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#Fig3">3</a>), Ti, O and C elements could be observed from the survey spectra in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#Fig3">3</a>a. For the further analysis of the chemical structure of the investigated TiO<sub>2</sub> samples, high-resolution XPS spectra of all elements are processed. Figure&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#Fig3">3</a>b shows the high-resolution spectra of Ti 2p, from the spectra we could see there are two typical peaks which centered at 464.3 and 458.5&nbsp;eV for all samples. They could be attributed to the Ti 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Ti 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, respectively, which represent the characteristic peaks of Ti<sup>4+</sup>. What’s more, compared to the other two samples, the Ti 2p<sub>3/2</sub> peak of H-TiO<sub>2</sub> shifted to the lower binding energy slightly, but the peak difference is so weak and we would say that the change of chemical environment of titanium is not obvious after high-power plasma treatment.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Figure 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-020-68838-x/MediaObjects/41598_2020_68838_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig3" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-020-68838-x/MediaObjects/41598_2020_68838_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure 3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="577"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) XPS survey spectra of TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes; (<b>b</b>) Ti 2p spectra of TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes; (<b>c</b>) O 1s spectra of TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes; (<b>d</b>) N 1s spectrum of H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 3" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
<p>Figure&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#Fig3">3</a>c shows the XPS spectra of O 1s, there is one main peak located at 529.7&nbsp;eV for the pristine and hydrogen treated materials, which is attributed to bulk oxygen O<sup>2−</sup>. For H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub>, two new characteristic peaks appear after plasma treatment, which are centered at 530.2 and 531.9&nbsp;eV, corresponding to the chemisorbed oxygen of the surface hydroxyl, absorbed H<sub>2</sub>O, absorbed O<sub>2</sub> or the surface contamination<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Singhal, R. K., Kumar, S., Kumari, P., Xing, Y. T. &amp; Saitovitch, E. Evidence of defect-induced ferromagnetism and its ‘switch’ action in pristine bulk TiO2. Appl. Phys. Lett. 98, 1–4 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d229305e959">49</a></sup>. Further, the peak centered at 531.9&nbsp;eV is attributed to the presence of Ti–O–N bonds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Singhal, R. K., Kumar, S., Kumari, P., Xing, Y. T. &amp; Saitovitch, E. Evidence of defect-induced ferromagnetism and its ‘switch’ action in pristine bulk TiO2. Appl. Phys. Lett. 98, 1–4 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d229305e963">49</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Battiston, G. A. et al. PECVD of amorphous TiO2 thin films: effect of growth temperature and plasma gas composition. Thin Solid Films 371, 126–131 (2000)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d229305e966">50</a></sup>, which indicates the formation of Ti–O–N bonds after the hydrogen and nitrogen plasma treatment.</p><p>In addition, the high-resolution XPS spectrum of N 1s of H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub> is displayed in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#Fig3">3</a>d. In accordance with a previous report<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Wang, H. et al. N-doped TiO2 with a disordered surface layer fabricated via plasma treatment as an anode with clearly enhanced performance for rechargeable sodium ion batteries. Sustain. Energy Fuels 3, 2688–2696 (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d229305e979">32</a></sup>, the 30-min nitrogen plasma treatment can result in about 0.5&nbsp;at% of nitrogen doping in the material. There are four characteristic peaks, which are located at 395.9, 399.5, 402.3 and 409.1&nbsp;eV, respectively. Firstly, according to some reports<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Chen, X. &amp; Burda, C. Photoelectron spectroscopic investigation of nitrogen-doped titania nanoparticles. J. Phys. Chem. B 108, 15446–15449 (2004)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d229305e983">51</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Sakthivel, S., Janczarek, M. &amp; Kisch, H. Visible light activity and photoelectrochemical properties of nitrogen-doped TiO2. J. Phys. Chem. B 108, 19384–19387 (2004)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d229305e986">52</a></sup> the peak of N 1s located at 395.9&nbsp;eV can be attributed to O–Ti–N linkages, which comes from the doping of N atoms into the TiO<sub>2</sub> lattice. Another peak at 399.4&nbsp;eV can be attributed to interstitial N<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Singhal, R. K., Kumar, S., Kumari, P., Xing, Y. T. &amp; Saitovitch, E. Evidence of defect-induced ferromagnetism and its ‘switch’ action in pristine bulk TiO2. Appl. Phys. Lett. 98, 1–4 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d229305e993">49</a></sup>. Furthermore, two other peaks at 402.3 and 409.8&nbsp;eV belong to NO or NO<sub>2</sub> type species which usually appear at binding energies higher than 400&nbsp;eV<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Battiston, G. A. et al. PECVD of amorphous TiO2 thin films: effect of growth temperature and plasma gas composition. Thin Solid Films 371, 126–131 (2000)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d229305e999">50</a></sup>. From the above observations, the chemical states of the nitrogen doped TiO<sub>2</sub> exist with the form of N–Ti–O and Ti–O–N<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Hu, Y. et al. Synthesis and photoelectrochemical response of CdS quantum dot-sensitized TiO2 nanorod array photoelectrodes. Nanoscale Res. Lett. 8, 1–5 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d229305e1005">46</a></sup>. Many research works have been reported that the role of N species on the electrical conductivity of TiO<sub>2</sub> is mainly related to the decrement of the band gap, because nitrogen doping can elevate the valence band maximum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Cong, Y., Zhang, J., Chen, F. &amp; Anpo, M. Synthesis and characterization of nitrogen-doped TiO2 nanophotocatalyst with high visible light activity. J. Phys. Chem. C 111, 6976–6982 (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d229305e1012">53</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Electrochemical performances of sodium ion batteries"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Electrochemical performances of sodium ion batteries</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>The electrochemical performances of the TiO<sub>2</sub> employed as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries were evaluated in coin half-cells using sodium metal as the counter electrode. Firstly, the cycle voltammetry (CV) plots which were evaluated at a scan rate of 0.5&nbsp;mV/s at a voltage range of 0.01–3.0&nbsp;V for 3 cycles of each sample are shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#Fig4">4</a>. All of the TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes display the typical CV curves of TiO<sub>2</sub> anode for SIBs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Wu, L., Buchholz, D., Bresser, D., Gomes Chagas, L. &amp; Passerini, S. Anatase TiO2 nanoparticles for high power sodium-ion anodes. J. Power Sources 251, 379–385 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d229305e1033">29</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Oh, S. M. et al. High electrochemical performances of microsphere C-TiO2 anode for sodium-ion battery. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces 6, 11295–11301 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d229305e1036">54</a></sup>. From the first cycle, irreversible broad cathode peaks at wide potential range of 0.01–0.5&nbsp;V are observed for all samples (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#Fig4">4</a>), which can be attributed to the irreversible sites for Na-ion insertion in the crystal lattice defects, electrolyte and other organic material decomposition and the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer formation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Oh, S. M. et al. High electrochemical performances of microsphere C-TiO2 anode for sodium-ion battery. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces 6, 11295–11301 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d229305e1044">54</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Kim, K. T. et al. Anatase titania nanorods as an intercalation anode material for rechargeable sodium batteries. Nano Lett. 14, 416–422 (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d229305e1047">55</a></sup>. From the second and third cycles, there are only the peaks at around 0.78 and 0.85&nbsp;V are shown in the CV profiles for all obtained samples (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#Fig4">4</a>), which are corresponding to the reversible insertion/de-insertion of Na into/from the electrodes. Moreover, except the first cycle, the CV curves of all three samples overlapped well for the second and third cycles, which demonstrated good cycling stability and the high reversibility of TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes. What’s more, the peak current of the H-TiO<sub>2</sub> and H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub> are wider and stronger than those of the P-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode, and it means the plasma treated materials show better sodium storage performances (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#Fig4">4</a>b,c).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Figure 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-020-68838-x/MediaObjects/41598_2020_68838_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig4" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-020-68838-x/MediaObjects/41598_2020_68838_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure 4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="575"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) CV curves of TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes measured at a scan rate of 0.5&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>-1</sup> for 3 cycles, (<b>a</b>) P-TiO<sub>2</sub>; (<b>b</b>) H-TiO<sub>2</sub>; (<b>c</b>) H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 4" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
<p>The rate performances of all samples are given in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#Fig5">5</a>a, b, all obtained samples were measured at current densities of 0.1, 0.5, 1, 2 and 5 C for 4 cycles of every step, and then the current densities came back to 0.1 C for 10 cycles (1C = 335&nbsp;mA/g). The first cycle discharge/ charge specific capacities of P-TiO<sub>2</sub>, H-TiO<sub>2</sub> and H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes, are 584.28/112.08, 562.86/117.70 and 603.60/121.65&nbsp;mAh/g, respectively. A very low coulombic efficiency for about 20% are obtained for all the materials at the first cycles. The irreversible capacities of the electrodes were mainly caused by the occurrence of the side reactions which could form a solid-electrolyte interface (SEI) layer. It is hard to say which treated material shown a better electrochemical performance, but we are sure that the plasma treatment methods have a good effect on the TiO<sub>2</sub> anode materials for SIBs. In order to further understand the improved higher performance of the TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes, electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) was evaluated in the frequency range of 100&nbsp;kHz to 0.01&nbsp;Hz (as shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#Fig5">5</a>c). The spectra were fitted by Zview software using an equivalent circuit mode. Here, R<sub>s</sub> is the internal resistance in the batteries and R<sub>1</sub> is charge transfer resistance on the interface of electrode and electrolyte. CPE represent a constant phase element and the sloping line in the low-frequency region related to W1, which is called Warburg impendence, could be attributed to the diffusion resistance in the electrode<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Lin, J. et al. In situ nitrogen doping of TiO2 by plasma enhanced atomic layer deposition for enhanced sodium storage performance. Dalton Trans. 46, 13101–13107 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d229305e1122">56</a></sup>. The charge transfer resistance of the P-TiO<sub>2</sub> is about 137&nbsp;Ω according to the fitted result, and increased a bit for the H-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode. While the charge transfer resistance of H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub> (ca. 66&nbsp;Ω) is about half of the P-TiO<sub>2</sub>, which means that the nitrogen doping is an easy and effective way to improve the electrical conductivity and the charge transfer reactions of the TiO<sub>2</sub> anode material for sodium ion batteries. From our previous work, an individual nitrogen plasma treated TiO<sub>2</sub> shown the similar result<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Wang, H. et al. N-doped TiO2 with a disordered surface layer fabricated via plasma treatment as an anode with clearly enhanced performance for rechargeable sodium ion batteries. Sustain. Energy Fuels 3, 2688–2696 (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d229305e1139">32</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Figure 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-020-68838-x/MediaObjects/41598_2020_68838_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig5" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-020-68838-x/MediaObjects/41598_2020_68838_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure 5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="538"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Rate performances of TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes at the current densities of 0.1, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0 and 5.0 C, (<b>a</b>) charge, (<b>b</b>) discharge; (<b>c</b>) Nyquist plots of TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 5" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
<p>The long cycling stabilities of the materials were evaluated at a current rate of 1 C for 400 cycles. As is shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#Fig6">6</a>a,c, the first discharge/charge specific capacities of P-TiO<sub>2</sub>, H-TiO<sub>2</sub> and H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode are 304.73/64.23, 325.76/69.37 and 338.07/72.54&nbsp;mAh/g, respectively. It is very similar to the rate performance, all of the materials show a very low Coulombic efficiency in the first cycle, about 21%. The low Coulombic efficiency of all samples is due to the large ion radius of sodium and low theoretical capacity of TiO<sub>2</sub> anodes, which can result in high irreversible capacities of anodes for sodium ion batteries. Secondly, the TiO<sub>2</sub> materials we used in this work is just normal nanoparticles, we don’t have special structure to provide large amounts of active sites for the electrochemical reactions. In addition, the nitrogen concentration after plasma treatment is also very low compared with some previous reported works<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Liu, S., Cai, Z., Zhou, J., Pan, A. &amp; Liang, S. Nitrogen-doped TiO2 nanospheres for advanced sodium-ion battery and sodium-ion capacitor applications. J. Mater. Chem. A 4, 18278–18283 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d229305e1183">37</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Wu, Y., Liu, X., Yang, Z., Gu, L. &amp; Yu, Y. Nitrogen-doped ordered mesoporous anatase TiO2 nanofibers as anode materials for high performance sodium-ion batteries. Small 
https://doi.org/10.1002/smll.201670126

 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d229305e1186">57</a></sup>, but we could still see the big improvement of the materials as anode with the effect of plasma treatment. After about 20 cycles, the materials which were treated with hydrogen and nitrogen plasma, show higher capacities than the pristine TiO<sub>2</sub>. Especially for the H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub>, the discharge/charge capacities for the 400th cycles are 68.05/66.85&nbsp;mAh/g, while the P-TiO<sub>2</sub> just show specific capacities of 47.40/46.44&nbsp;mAh/g, it means that H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub> showing a 43.5% of capacity higher than the P-TiO<sub>2</sub> after 400 long-term cycles.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Figure 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-020-68838-x/MediaObjects/41598_2020_68838_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig6" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-020-68838-x/MediaObjects/41598_2020_68838_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure 6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="814"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Long term cycling performances of electrodes at a current rate of 1 C (<b>a</b>) discharge; (<b>c</b>) charge; Galvanostatic discharge/charge curves of electrodes at different cycles (<b>b</b>) P-TiO<sub>2</sub>; (d) H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub>; (<b>e</b>) Coulombic efficiencies for long cycling performances of P-TiO<sub>2</sub> and H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 6" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
<p>Compared with our previous work<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Wang, H. et al. N-doped TiO2 with a disordered surface layer fabricated via plasma treatment as an anode with clearly enhanced performance for rechargeable sodium ion batteries. Sustain. Energy Fuels 3, 2688–2696 (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d229305e1234">32</a></sup>, hydrogen and nitrogen double plasma treatment give an improvement on the specific capacities (increased around 10% at 400th cycle) than an individual nitrogen plasma treatment, it means the hydrogenation process also makes contributions on enhancing the sodium storage performances. All of the samples show a good long cycling stability, the Coulombic efficiencies of them are nearly 99% after 50 cycles, and this could be sustained to the end of long cycling (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#Fig6">6</a>e). In addition, there are some fluctuations of the capacity upon the further cycling for all samples. The reason is not clear. However, Wu<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Wu, C. et al. Insight into the origin of capacity fluctuation of Na2Ti6O13 anode in sodium ion batteries. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces 9, 43596–43602 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d229305e1241">58</a></sup> and co-workers reported that during the reaction of the large size of sodium ions with the electrodes, more electrolyte penetrates from the surface of electrode into the bulk and then more active materials are participated into the reaction with the increasing of the cycling numbers; also, some other reported works<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Soto, F. A. et al. Tuning the solid electrolyte interphase for selective Li- and Na-ion storage in hard carbon. Adv. Mater. 29, 1606860 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d229305e1245">59</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Cui, X. et al. Improving Mn tolerance of lithium-ion batteries by using lithium bis(oxalato)borate-based electrolyte. Electrochim. Acta 253, 291–301 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d229305e1248">60</a></sup> claim that the deposition of transition metal compounds on the anode electrode would induce the chemical degradation of SEI film. When the accumulation of produced metallic titanium reached to a certain amount, metallic titanium may catalyze and accelerate the decomposition of SEI film with the electrolyte, which could cause a sudden rise of specific capacity of electrodes.</p><p>Because the different performances of the P-TiO<sub>2</sub> and H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub> during the long-term cycling process, so we want to get some more details from the long-term performance galvanostatic discharge and charge curves, as shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#Fig6">6</a>b,d. The first cycle of these two materials are very similar for the charge and discharge process. But after several cycles, we could see that something is different for the charge curves. The H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub> shown a slow potential increase from ~ 1.0 to 2.0&nbsp;V (as shown by the red circle in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#Fig6">6</a>d), it may cause by the amorphous surface layer which is good for the sodium ions transport in the material and also the nitrogen doping could greatly increase the conductivity of the TiO<sub>2</sub> materials. Figure&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#Fig7">7</a>a is the 1st and 21st galvanostatic discharge/charge curve of P-TiO<sub>2</sub> and H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes at the current density of 0.1 C. The 1st discharge curve of H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub> indicate a longer voltage plateau from 0.5 to 0.01&nbsp;V compared to P-TiO<sub>2</sub>, which is matched well with the CV results and also tell us that H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub> possesses a higher sodium ion storage ability. The 21st cycle galvanostatic discharge/charge curves show the similar result. According to the results mentioned above, firstly, the achieved discharge specific capacity of H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub> is higher than the P-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode; secondly, for H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub>, the higher charge capacities could be obtained when the charge voltage is higher than 1.0&nbsp;V, means that the reversible charge capacities of H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub> are higher than the P-TiO<sub>2</sub>. In addition, Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x#Fig7">7</a>b, c) show the galvanostatic discharge/charge curves of the electrodes at different current densities. The discharge/charge capacities of H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub> are all higher than that of P-TiO<sub>2</sub>. And much slower performance fading of H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub> is obtained at all different current stages. All these results give solid proofs of the considerable performance improvement of the H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Figure 7"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 7</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-020-68838-x/MediaObjects/41598_2020_68838_Fig7_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig7" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-020-68838-x/MediaObjects/41598_2020_68838_Fig7_HTML.png" alt="figure 7" loading="lazy" width="685" height="541"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Galvanostatic discharge/charge curves of P-TiO<sub>2</sub> and H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes at current density of 0.1 C. Galvanostatic discharge/charge curves at different current densities (<b>b</b>) P-TiO<sub>2</sub>, (<b>c</b>) H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-020-68838-x/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 7" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
</div></div></section><section data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>In summary, we developed a combination of hydrogen and nitrogen plasma assisted strategy to synthesize nitrogen doping and defect-rich TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode that demonstrated an enhanced performance as anode material of sodium ion batteries. When used as anode materials, the H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub> shown the best sodium storage performance, and then is H-TiO<sub>2</sub>, both of them demonstrated much higher specific capacities than pristine TiO<sub>2</sub>. The rate performances of H-TiO<sub>2</sub> and H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub> are significantly improved compared to P-TiO<sub>2</sub>. What’s more, H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub> shows a 43.5% of capacity higher than the P-TiO<sub>2</sub> after 400 cycles long-term discharge/charge process, and the samples show a good long cycling stability as well, the Coulombic efficiencies of all samples are nearly 99% after 50 cycles, and this could be sustained to the end of long cycling. In addition, H–N-TiO<sub>2</sub> reached the stable high Coulombic efficiency earlier than the pristine material. The high-resolution images of transmission electrons microscopy shown that there is a disordered surface layer was formed after the plasma treated materials, no matter the hydrogen or nitrogen plasma process. For this improvement of the electrodes, the disordered surface layer, the oxygen vacancies and the nitrogen doping play significant roles&nbsp;together in enhancing the electrochemical sodium storage performance.</p></div></div></section>
                    
                </div>
